<div id="review_128576365" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/128576365" />
    <a title="Josiah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1914201-josiah"><img alt="Josiah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1239682454p2/1914201.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/128576365?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josiah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josiah" href="/user/show/1914201-josiah">Josiah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780440944591" href="/book/show/415001.The_Chocolate_War">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer128576365" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer774064003958399044">The master. The greatest. What can I say about Robert Cormier that so many before have not already declared? Yet it still seems to fall short of the mark. There is an internal tenseness that one gets when even thinking about reading a Robert Cormier book, that is unlike the effect of any other writer. You can't count on everything turning out all right in the end, the protagonist being led through extended difficulties to a place of greater knowledge and peace with themselves. You really never k</span>
  <span id="freeText774064003958399044" style="display:none">The master. The greatest. What can I say about Robert Cormier that so many before have not already declared? Yet it still seems to fall short of the mark. There is an internal tenseness that one gets when even thinking about reading a Robert Cormier book, that is unlike the effect of any other writer. You can't count on everything turning out all right in the end, the protagonist being led through extended difficulties to a place of greater knowledge and peace with themselves. You really never know what is going to happen in a Robert Cormier story until you've read the final word, that bone-chilling moment when it all clicks into place and The Master releases you from his grasp.<br /><br /> <i>The Chocolate War</i> is one of those books that are frequently pointed to as an example of how easy it is for publishers to miss out on a rare literary jewel; reportedly, it was turned down by a long line of publishers before someone astute decided that it was a good investment to bet on this gripping, edgy story and its author Robert Cormier. Since then, it has sold staggering numbers of copies and is considered a classic of young adult lit.<br /><br /> The theme of <i>The Chocolate War</i> isn't really obvious. Robert Cormier never compromises gritty realism for the sake of tying up a neat lesson with a fancy ribbon, but instead lets us peer into the murky darkness of human nature for ourselves and learn whatever we might be ready to learn from the story. Still, I would say that more than anything else, <i>The Chocolate War</i> is a discomforting and electrifying warning: "disturb(ing) the universe" might seem like an attractive concept to us at times, but doing so comes with a forbidding price tag, a price tag that increases with just how extensively we want to make our mark. In the words of one character in the book, "They tell you to do your thing but they don't mean it. They don't want you to do your thing, not unless it happens to be their thing, too." We're encouraged as children to go out some day and really change the world, yet we have no idea how bad it can get if we follow through with our aspirations. But Robert Cormier knows...<br /><br /> As I progressed through <i>The Chocolate War</i>, the feeling within me never let up that every page I turned was taking me closer to impending cataclysm, and not in the same cathartic way as most books. In Robert Cormier novels, the sense of doom feels sickeningly real, nearly as upsetting to await and behold as if I were actually there in the story. It's spellbinding and haunting and beautiful and horrible, and it's almost impossible to describe to anyone who hasn't read the books for themselves. <i>The Chocolate War</i> is one of Robert Cormier's most resounding works, in my opinion, a potential mind-changer and life-changer that I'm sure will never lose its raw power through the passage of time. I would give it at least three and a half stars.</span>
  <a data-text-id="774064003958399044" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText7166925702362822783" style="display:none">Reading this for/with the 8th grade. Mrs. LeVasseur had a pile of them and she highly recommended it. "Sure!" I says. <br /><br />SO now I'm well more than half way and fascinated. I keep thinking that it's some big analogy for government and democracies or maybe the school is Russia and it's about communism. I'll have to check when it was written.<br /><br />And now that I'm finished with it...let me continue my review:<br /><br />I'm really surprised by the complexity of the characters. There are several entities that seem to control the masses and the masses do indeed follow ever whim of the leaders. Very scary. 1984-esque, I would say.           <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780440944591" href="/book/show/415001.The_Chocolate_War">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer14959159" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15013934668954518106">I Did not like this book at all. I had to force myself to finish it just because I hate leaving books unfinished. It is a story about a chocolate sale at a private boys prep school. The action revolves around one evil bully, an equally evil and manipulative teacher and their victim. I find it extremely unbelievable that one teenage boy could have as much power as the bully in this story does. The plot was completely ridiculous. I did not care for the theme of the book or most of the action of th</span>
  <span id="freeText15013934668954518106" style="display:none">I Did not like this book at all. I had to force myself to finish it just because I hate leaving books unfinished. It is a story about a chocolate sale at a private boys prep school. The action revolves around one evil bully, an equally evil and manipulative teacher and their victim. I find it extremely unbelievable that one teenage boy could have as much power as the bully in this story does. The plot was completely ridiculous. I did not care for the theme of the book or most of the action of the book. Much of it was obscene and lacked any good morals what so ever. After reading this cynical and dark story I need to go find something light, fun and easy to read just so I can wash this book out of my brain.<br />Don't waste your time. Read something better.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15013934668954518106" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_15077416" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15077416" />
    <a title="Nova" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/415101-nova"><img alt="Nova" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1190691387p2/415101.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15077416?book_show_action=true">Feb 10, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nova" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nova" href="/user/show/415101-nova">Nova</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/415101-nova?shelf=library-materials">library-materials</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer15077416" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5490153942237468276">The Chocolate War, by Robert Cormier, takes place in a Catholic prep school for boys. The main character is Jerry Renault, a freshman who is dealing with the recent death of his mother. Not only has Jerry’s mother died, but his father has become very depressed and is unable to help Jerry through this difficult time. When school starts Jerry puts everything he has into making it on the football team, and things seem to be going well until the lead member of the school’s secret society, The Vigils</span>
  <span id="freeText5490153942237468276" style="display:none">The Chocolate War, by Robert Cormier, takes place in a Catholic prep school for boys. The main character is Jerry Renault, a freshman who is dealing with the recent death of his mother. Not only has Jerry’s mother died, but his father has become very depressed and is unable to help Jerry through this difficult time. When school starts Jerry puts everything he has into making it on the football team, and things seem to be going well until the lead member of the school’s secret society, The Vigils, singles him out for a difficult assignment. Archie, the ring leader of The Vigils, decides that Jerry must refuse to sell chocolates for the big school fundraiser, in order to stay out of trouble with the dangerous secret society. When Jerry refuses to sell the chocolates Brother Leon, a cruel and frightening teacher at the school, makes things very uncomfortable for Jerry. <br />The theme of this novel is depicted plainly by the quote on the poster in Jerry’s locker; do I dare disturb the universe? Jerry is dealing with the depression at home, and with the realization that most of the teachers and the students at his school are bad people. When The Vigils tell Jerry that his assignment is over and that he can now sell the chocolates, Jerry performs an act of rebellion in the school and still refuses to sell the chocolates. It is at this point that things really begin to fall a part for Jerry, and he is subjected to fierce bullying by classmates and teachers because of his rebellion. The most difficult thing about this novel is that it ends on such a desperate note. Jerry has been broken by The Vigil, and the members get by with no punishment. Robert Cormier writes a follow up to this story called Beyond the Chocolate War, but I have not read it. I am interested to see if justice is served in the sequel. <br /><br />I think this is the kind of story that speaks to high school students during a trying time in their life. Unfortunately, many students would find the fact that the bullies go unpunished quite realistic. This story could be used in a high school English class for a novel study, and would also be a great book to recommend to individual readers. Even though I think girls could be interested in the characters, this book seems like it is really meant for male readers. Boys and men dominate this story; women serve only as sexual objects of desire, not real people. <br />This book was published in 1974 and from the beginning it has faced opposition. The Chocolate War has been challenged because of sexual content, inappropriate language, and violence. I though the language and violence was fairly mild, but throughout the story masturbation is alluded to several times. I think this novel is appropriate for high school aged students. <br />The Chocolate War was written such a long time ago that I had a difficult time finding comprehensive book reviews, but I did find some quotes from book reviews on the Random House Web site. The reviewer from School Library Journal thought that The Chocolate War was “an uncompromising portrait of human cruelty and conformity” and the reviewer from The New York Times Book Review wrote that “The Chocolate War is masterfully structured and rich in theme”. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5490153942237468276" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12540485666567122390">I don't <s>recommend</s> <a href='http://bookchilla.wordpress.com/2012/04/12/book-review-the-chocolate-war-by-robert-cormier/' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Chocolate War</a>.<br/><br/>*****<br/><br/>I guess there is no way to soften the blow, but <i>The Chocolate War</i> is senseless to me. i thought the book has no purpose. It is my first time to encounter a story without a likely hero. Man, this really sucked for me! My apologies to my dear GR friends who liked <i>The Chocolate War</i>. Like what i said, agree to disagree. right? =)<br/><br/>I guess what i'm looking for is this: a ray of hope, a sliver or chance that maybe, maybe in the end something good will happen. How</span>
  <span id="freeText12540485666567122390" style="display:none">I don't <s>recommend</s> <a href="http://bookchilla.wordpress.com/2012/04/12/book-review-the-chocolate-war-by-robert-cormier/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Chocolate War</a>.<br /><br />*****<br /><br />I guess there is no way to soften the blow, but <i>The Chocolate War</i> is senseless to me. i thought the book has no purpose. It is my first time to encounter a story without a likely hero. Man, this really sucked for me! My apologies to my dear GR friends who liked <i>The Chocolate War</i>. Like what i said, agree to disagree. right? =)<br /><br />I guess what i'm looking for is this: a ray of hope, a sliver or chance that maybe, maybe in the end something good will happen. How i believe in karma! it was unfortunate that such karma is beyond reach to <b>Jerry</b>, <b>Goober</b>, and to anyone else who felt oppressed, physically or psychologically, in this book.<br /><br />i hate bullies. and i believe bullying was glorified in the end. what was all the sacrifices for, if not to wholly succeed after the debacle? Yep, the story was SENSELESS. <br /><br />A book doesn't always have to have a happy ending, but my gosh, <i>The Chocolate War</i> did not give me even a little morsel to cheer on after i read it! Ugh. Bad chocolate taste.<br /><br />YA books should empower its teen readers, and not suffocate them with an ugly truth without telling them that they CAN survive the harshness of life.<br /><br />*Sniffs* can someone send me a MARS chocolate bar to ease my bitterness? =)</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14469004024334071009"><b>Robert Cormier</b><br/>The Chocolate War<br/>Ember<br/>267 pages<br/>6.3<br/><br/>A glowing review from <i>The New York Times</i> doesn't pretty much guarantee that that book will fulfill your high expectation. Instead, it rockets your expectation high, and it hurts when your expectation fails you. Robert Cormier's <i>The Chocolate War</i>, for example, is often regarded as one of the best young adult books ever written, but for me, <i>The Chocolate War</i> is a homophobic and misogynistic book, set in 1970s in all-boys Catholic high school--which m</span>
  <span id="freeText14469004024334071009" style="display:none"><b>Robert Cormier</b><br />The Chocolate War<br />Ember<br />267 pages<br />6.3<br /><br />A glowing review from <i>The New York Times</i> doesn't pretty much guarantee that that book will fulfill your high expectation. Instead, it rockets your expectation high, and it hurts when your expectation fails you. Robert Cormier's <i>The Chocolate War</i>, for example, is often regarded as one of the best young adult books ever written, but for me, <i>The Chocolate War</i> is a homophobic and misogynistic book, set in 1970s in all-boys Catholic high school--which means a perfect formula for homophobic and misogynistic book. The characters' back story are unfocused and blurry. Indeed, Cormier's a great writer. His sentences are all raw and trenchant, provoking young souls' mind and their conformity, a reminder that sometimes life sucks. And that's probably the only great thing about <i>The Chocolate War</i>. </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1814659309" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14771090732460467493">“They don't actually want you to do your own thing, not unless it's their thing too.” <br/>― Robert Cormier, The Chocolate War<br/><br/><br/><br/><br/>A great book from childhood. It is amazing that a decision not to sell chocolate could be the setup for such an incredible meaningful book. This is also a movie.<br/><br/>Highly recommended.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_39822765" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/39822765" />
    <a title="Kandice" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1396160-kandice"><img alt="Kandice" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1231810788p2/1396160.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/39822765?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kandice" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kandice" href="/user/show/1396160-kandice">Kandice</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39822765" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12658921343890304149">Trinity is an all boy Catholic school. As part of an annual Trinity fundraiser Brother Leon has pledged the boys to sell 20,000 boxes of chocolate, up 100% from the normal 10,000 boxes goal. Not only that, the chocolates cost $2 instead of the usual $1. <br/><br/>Trinity is ruled by an elite clique, The Vigils, and the person who runs that clique is Archie Costello. Archie creates “assignments” for his fellow students: various stunts and pranks.<br/><br/>Jerry is a Trinity freshman. Archie’s assignment to Jerry is </span>
  <span id="freeText12658921343890304149" style="display:none">Trinity is an all boy Catholic school. As part of an annual Trinity fundraiser Brother Leon has pledged the boys to sell 20,000 boxes of chocolate, up 100% from the normal 10,000 boxes goal. Not only that, the chocolates cost $2 instead of the usual $1. <br /><br />Trinity is ruled by an elite clique, The Vigils, and the person who runs that clique is Archie Costello. Archie creates “assignments” for his fellow students: various stunts and pranks.<br /><br />Jerry is a Trinity freshman. Archie’s assignment to Jerry is simple: say “no” to selling chocolate for two weeks, then say “yes.” That’s it, but Jerry never says yes. At the same time Archie’s telling Jerry not to sell chocolates, he’s told Brother Leon that he’s going to get The Vigils to make this the best chocolate sale ever. His fellow Vigils aren’t too thrilled with Archie because this is a hard order to fill and The Vigils don’t fail.<br /><br />Jerry is supposed to be the main character. It’s his refusal to sell chocolate that begins and propels the plot, and we are even given a look at Jerry’s life outside of school: a dead mother, a busy father, a girl he may like. And yet, when it comes to one of the most critical points in the book — Jerry being assigned to say “no” and saying “no” — Jerry is absent. His viewpoint isn’t shared. We don’t get his view again until he decides to say “no” after Archie tells him to say “yes.”<br /><br />For me, Archie seems to be the main character. He is the puppet master, and even though we learn nothing personal about him, i.e. parents, siblings, social status, everything that happens can, in some way, everything that happens can be traced back to Archie. I like how clearly his character is drawn. He is a manipulator; he is the smartest person in the room; he totally lacks empathy. He’s either going to be a fantastic success or a serial killer.<br /><br />Trinity boys do what Archie tells them to do, always, so how does Jerry resist? I can see Archie leading Jerry to a place of “no.” And this is what I loved about The Chocolate War: because of the different, even unreliable voices telling the story, we can’t really know what each character thought, meant, intended, or even believed in. We are given a few details of Jerry’s life to lull us into believing that not selling the chocolate is a protest on jerry’s part. I think he decides not to sell the chocolates, even when finally told to do so, is because Archie has led him to that decision.<br /><br />The book ends with an incredibly violent and visceral scene. One which made my stomach turn to read. By making Archie a sociopath without remorse, the reader, who is shocked by the end, who is feeling empathy, can say to themselves, I’m better than Archie because I wouldn’t go that far. I would feel terrible. That’s the real genius of this book. Not being told what the characters do, but allowing us to tell ourselves what we would not. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12658921343890304149" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1328385109" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1328385109" />
    <a title="Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/100276-jean"><img alt="Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1184181577p2/100276.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1328385109?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jean" href="/user/show/100276-jean">Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1328385109" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer643019897223230028">Honestly, I didn't expect or even really want to like this, let alone love it. I picked it up because it's sort of a classic and references my favorite poem and allegedly is a great "boy book." More of a great human book. As disturbing as Lord of the Flies, yet set in circumstances so familiar and accessible that the horror is even more plausible and visceral. For me, it brought up former high school stuff and current teacher stuff. Beyond that, though, it is just so incredibly insightful and ar</span>
  <span id="freeText643019897223230028" style="display:none">Honestly, I didn't expect or even really want to like this, let alone love it. I picked it up because it's sort of a classic and references my favorite poem and allegedly is a great "boy book." More of a great human book. As disturbing as Lord of the Flies, yet set in circumstances so familiar and accessible that the horror is even more plausible and visceral. For me, it brought up former high school stuff and current teacher stuff. Beyond that, though, it is just so incredibly insightful and artfully constructed in its presentation of human nature. It humanizes every character, making everyone understandable and identifiable before dehumanizing them with stunning ease. The monstrous ending is notable for the fact that it's peopled by people. KID people, not monsters. <br /><br />"Do I dare disturb the universe?" Maybe my favorite part about this book is that it realistically portrayed how our own universe is constructed not by reality, but rather our perception of it. When that's the case, wars ensue over even something as simple and sweet as chocolate, and probably 90% of human drama is chocolate wars. I know that most find this book pessimistic and dark, but I found it the opposite. The only way to avoid the darkness is to disturb it unrelentingly.</span>
  <a data-text-id="643019897223230028" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1585516658" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4661473787368543653">Pat Benatar got it all wrong. Hell isn't for children, it's for teenagers.<br/>Having spent my teenage years in a religious school for boys I can honestly say Robert Cormier got it down perfectly, the endless abuse from teachers and classmates alike, abuse given with lip-licking relish. The Chocolate War really took me back. Good thing the novel finally ended or I would have had a breakdown! <br/>The great irony to this testimony to educational tyranny and manipulation is that several schools in the count</span>
  <span id="freeText4661473787368543653" style="display:none">Pat Benatar got it all wrong. Hell isn't for children, it's for teenagers.<br />Having spent my teenage years in a religious school for boys I can honestly say Robert Cormier got it down perfectly, the endless abuse from teachers and classmates alike, abuse given with lip-licking relish. The Chocolate War really took me back. Good thing the novel finally ended or I would have had a breakdown! <br />The great irony to this testimony to educational tyranny and manipulation is that several schools in the country tried to ban this great work. Ha!<br />This book was written while America was swooning to shit like The Brady Bunch and The Partridge Family. This novel was the flip side to all that nonsense, the Real Teen Despair, my Seventies teenage years. Robert Cormier lives! </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('/oVC2FuKqds6I6+xULoXTOp9WkkEPMqKY+myRGYkPQxZb1iPhC9DXDHhQ2eMsXqqkn4k3lbsqnGKt3XLVo12NQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Kgc01UprgSHy36YWIkxk6II8ZMs24VRNpr3hqkzlNCiN7S6Clc5rpvkdSsD+RwkO+j8aXGQxNLZP4yYlfEx/EQ==')})
  }

The boy is mauled by Apron, the neighbor\'s ailing cow whom he helps, alone, to give birth. The grateful farmer brings him a gift—a newborn pig. His father at first demurs (&quot;We thank you, Brother Tanner,&quot; said Papa, &quot;but it\'s not the Shaker Way to take frills for being neighborly. All that Robert done was what any farmer would do for another&quot;) but is persuaded. Rob keeps the pig, names her, and gives her his devotion... He wrestles with grammar in the schoolhouse. He hears rumors of sin. He is taken—at last—to the Rutland Fair. He broadens his heart to make room even for Baptists. And when his father, who can neither read nor cipher, whose hands are bloodied by his trade, whose wisdom and mastery of country things are bred in the bone, entrusts Rob with his final secret, the boy makes the sacrifice that completes his passage into manhood.\n\nAll is told with quiet humor and simplicity. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5827042133770608290">Robert Edmund Cormier (January 17, 1925–November 2, 2000) was an American author, columnist and reporter, known for his deeply pessimistic, downbeat literature. His most popular works include <i>I Am the Cheese, After the First Death, We All Fall Down </i>and <i>The Chocolate War</i>, all of which have won awards. <i>The Chocolate War </i>was challenged in multiple libraries. His books often are concerned with themes </span>
  <span id="freeText5827042133770608290" style="display:none">Robert Edmund Cormier (January 17, 1925–November 2, 2000) was an American author, columnist and reporter, known for his deeply pessimistic, downbeat literature. His most popular works include <i>I Am the Cheese, After the First Death, We All Fall Down </i>and <i>The Chocolate War</i>, all of which have won awards. <i>The Chocolate War </i>was challenged in multiple libraries. His books often are concerned with themes such as abuse, mental illness, violence, revenge, betrayal and conspiracy. In most of his novels, the protagonists do not win.</span>
